[2;1H       The  interim DNS update scheme was written before the RFCs were finalized and does not quite follow them.  The[m
[3;1H       RFCs call for a new DHCID RRtype while he interim DNS update scheme uses a TXT record.  In addition the  ddns-[m
[4;1H       resolution draft called for the DHCP server to put a DHCID RR on the PTR record, but the [4minterim[24m update method[m
[5;1H       does not do this.  In the final RFC this requirement was relaxed such that a server may add a DHCID RR to  the[m

[8;1H[1mDYNAMIC[0m [1mDNS[0m [1mUPDATE[0m [1mSECURITY[0m[m
[9;1H       When  you set your DNS server up to allow updates from the DHCP server, you may be exposing it to unauthorized[m
[10;1H       updates.  To avoid this, you should use TSIG signatures - a method of cryptographically signing updates  using[m
[11;1H       a  shared  secret  key.   As  long as you protect the secrecy of this key, your updates should also be secure.[m
[12;1H       Note, however, that the DHCP protocol itself provides no security, and  that  clients  can  therefore  provide[m
[13;1H       information  to  the  DHCP  server  which  the  DHCP server will then use in its updates, with the constraints[m
[14;1H       described previously.[m
[15;1H[m
[16;1H       The DNS server must be configured to allow updates for any zone that the DHCP server will  be  updating.   For[m
[17;1H       example,  let us say that clients in the sneedville.edu domain will be assigned addresses on the 10.10.17.0/24[m
[18;1H       subnet.  In that case, you will need a key declaration for the TSIG key you will be using, and also  two  zone[m
[19;1H       declarations  - one for the zone containing A records that will be updates and one for the zone containing PTR[m
[20;1H       records - for ISC BIND, something like this:[m
[21;1H[m
[22;1H       key DHCP_UPDATER {[m
[23;1H         algorithm hmac-md5;[m
[24;1H         secret pRP5FapFoJ95JEL06sv4PQ==;[m
[25;1H       };[m
[26;1H[m
[27;1H       zone "example.org" {[m
[28;1H            type master;[m
[29;1H            file "example.org.db";[m
[30;1H            allow-update { key DHCP_UPDATER; };[m
[31;1H       };[m
[32;1H[m
[33;1H       zone "17.10.10.in-addr.arpa" {[m
[34;1H            type master;[m
[35;1H            file "10.10.17.db";[m
[36;1H            allow-update { key DHCP_UPDATER; };[m
[37;1H       };[m
[38;1H[1;1H[m
[2;1H       The  interim DNS update scheme was written before the RFCs were finalized and does not quite follow them.  The[m
[3;1H       RFCs call for a new DHCID RRtype while he interim DNS update scheme uses a TXT record.  In addition the  ddns-[m
[4;1H       resolution draft called for the DHCP server to put a DHCID RR on the PTR record, but the [4minterim[24m update method[m
[5;1H       does not do this.  In the final RFC this requirement was relaxed such that a server may add a DHCID RR to  the[m
[6;1H       PTR record.[m
[7;1H[m

       [1mThe[0m [4mhost[24m [1mstatement[0m[m
[m
        [1mhost[0m [4mhostname[24m {[m
          [ [4mparameters[24m ][m
          [ [4mdeclarations[24m ][m
        [1m}[0m[m
[m
       The [1mhost[0m declaration provides a scope in which to provide configuration information about a  specific  client,[m
       and  also provides a way to assign a client a fixed address.  The host declaration provides a way for the DHCP[m
       server to identify a DHCP or BOOTP client, and also a way to assign the client a static IP address.[m
[m
       If it is desirable to be able to boot a DHCP or BOOTP client on more than one  subnet  with  fixed  addresses,[m
       more  than  one address may be specified in the [4mfixed-address[24m declaration, or more than one [1mhost[0m statement may[m
       be specified matching the same client.[m
[m
       If client-specific boot parameters must change based on the network to which the client is attached, then mul‚Äê[m
       tiple [1mhost[0m declarations should be used.  The [1mhost[0m declarations will only match a client if one of their [4mfixed-[24m[m
       [4maddress[24m statements is viable on the subnet (or shared network) where the client is attached.  Conversely,  for[m
       a  [1mhost[0m  declaration  to  match a client being allocated a dynamic address, it must not have any [4mfixed-address[24m[m
       statements.  You may therefore need a mixture of [1mhost[0m declarations for any given client...some  having  [4mfixed-[24m[m
       [4maddress[24m statements, others without.[m
[m
       [4mhostname[24m  should be a name identifying the host.  If a [4mhostname[24m option is not specified for the host, [4mhostname[24m[m
       is used.[m
[m
       [4mHost[24m declarations are matched to actual DHCP or BOOTP clients by matching the [4mdhcp-client-identifier[24m  or  [4mpxe-[24m[m
       [4mclient-id[24m  options specified in the [4mhost[24m declaration to the one supplied by the client, or, if the [4mhost[24m decla‚Äê[m
       ration or the client does not provide a [4mdhcp-client-identifier[24m or [4mpxe-client-id[24m options, by matching the [4mhard‚Äê[24m[m
       [4mware[24m  parameter in the [4mhost[24m declaration to the network hardware address supplied by the client.  BOOTP clients[m
       do not normally provide a [4mdhcp-client-identifier[24m, so the hardware address must be used for  all  clients  that[m
       may boot using the BOOTP protocol.[m
[m
       DHCPv6  servers  can  use the [4mhost-identifier[24m [4moption[24m parameter in the [4mhost[24m declaration, and specify any option[m
       with a fixed value to identify hosts.[m

       Notice that at the beginning of the file, there's a place for [7mglobal[27m parameters.  These might be  things  like[m
       the  organization's domain name, the addresses of the name servers (if they are common to the entire organiza‚Äê[m
       tion), and so on.  So, for example:[m
[m
            option domain-name "isc.org";[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 71/1945 5% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K [KESCESC[KOO[KAA[K[HM[m
[38;1H[K[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 70/1945 5% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K [KESCESC[KOO[KAA[K[HM       A typical dhcpd.conf file will look something like this:[m
[38;1H[K[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 69/1945 5% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[m
            option domain-name "isc.org";[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 71/1945 5% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K            option domain-name-servers ns1.isc.org, ns2.isc.org;[m
[m
                                                          Figure 2[m
[m
       As you can see in Figure 2, you can specify host addresses in parameters using their domain names rather  than[m
       their  numeric  IP  addresses.  If a given hostname resolves to more than one IP address (for example, if that[m
       host has two ethernet interfaces), then where possible, both addresses are supplied to the client.[m
[m
       The most obvious reason for having subnet-specific parameters as shown in Figure 1 is  that  each  subnet,  of[m
       necessity, has its own router.  So for the first subnet, for example, there should be something like:[m
[m
            option routers 204.254.239.1;[m
[m
       Note  that  the  address here is specified numerically.  This is not required - if you have a different domain[m
       name for each interface on your router, it's perfectly legitimate to use the domain name  for  that  interface[m
       instead  of  the  numeric  address.   However,  in  many  cases there may be only one domain name for all of a[m
       router's IP addresses, and it would not be appropriate to use that name here.[m
[m
       In Figure 1 there is also a [4mgroup[24m statement, which provides common parameters for  a  set  of  three  hosts  -[m
       zappo,  beppo  and  harpo.   As  you can see, these hosts are all in the test.isc.org domain, so it might make[m
       sense for a group-specific parameter to override the domain name supplied to these hosts:[m
[m
            option domain-name "test.isc.org";[m
[m
       Also, given the domain they're in, these are probably test machines.  If we wanted to test  the  DHCP  leasing[m
       mechanism, we might set the lease timeout somewhat shorter than the default:[m
[m
            max-lease-time 120;[m
            default-lease-time 120;[m
[m
       You may have noticed that while some parameters start with the [4moption[24m keyword, some do not.  Parameters start‚Äê[m
       ing with the [4moption[24m keyword correspond to actual DHCP options, while parameters that do  not  start  with  the[m

[?1049h[?1h=dhcpd.conf(5)                                    File Formats Manual                                    dhcpd.conf(5)[m

[1mNAME[0m[m
       dhcpd.conf - dhcpd configuration file[m
[m
[1mDESCRIPTION[0m[m
       The dhcpd.conf file contains configuration information for [4mdhcpd,[24m the Internet Systems Consortium DHCP Server.[m
[m
       The  dhcpd.conf  file is a free-form ASCII text file.  It is parsed by the recursive-descent parser built into[m
       dhcpd.  The file may contain extra tabs and newlines for formatting purposes.  Keywords in the file are  case-[m
       insensitive.  Comments may be placed anywhere within the file (except within quotes).  Comments begin with the[m
       # character and end at the end of the line.[m
[m
       The file essentially consists of a list of statements.  Statements fall into two broad categories - parameters[m
       and declarations.[m
[m
       Parameter statements either say how to do something (e.g., how long a lease to offer), whether to do something[m
       (e.g., should dhcpd provide addresses to unknown clients), or what parameters to provide to the client  (e.g.,[m
       use gateway 220.177.244.7).[m
[m
       Declarations  are used to describe the topology of the network, to describe clients on the network, to provide[m
       addresses that can be assigned to clients, or to apply a group of parameters to a group of  declarations.   In[m
       any  group  of  parameters  and  declarations,  all parameters must be specified before any declarations which[m
       depend on those parameters may be specified.[m
[m
       Declarations about network topology include the [4mshared-network[24m and the [4msubnet[24m declarations.  If clients  on  a[m
       subnet  are  to  be assigned addresses dynamically, a [4mrange[24m declaration must appear within the [4msubnet[24m declara‚Äê[m
       tion.  For clients with statically assigned addresses, or for installations where only known clients  will  be[m
       served, each such client must have a [4mhost[24m declaration.  If parameters are to be applied to a group of declara‚Äê[m
       tions which are not related strictly on a per-subnet basis, the [4mgroup[24m declaration can be used.[m
[m
       For every subnet which will be served, and for every subnet to which the dhcp server is connected, there  must[m
       be  one  [4msubnet[24m  declaration,  which tells dhcpd how to recognize that an address is on that subnet.  A [4msubnet[24m[m
       declaration is required for each subnet even if no addresses will be dynamically allocated on that subnet.[m
[m
       Some installations have physical networks on which more than one IP subnet operates.  For example, if there is[m
       a site-wide requirement that 8-bit subnet masks be used, but a department with a single physical ethernet net‚Äê[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 1 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[Kmm[Kaa[Kxx[K--[Kee[K[Kll[Kee[Kaa[Kss[Kee[K[1;1Hdhcpd.conf(5)                                    File Formats Manual                                    dhcpd.conf(5)[m

            [7mmax-lease[27m-time 120;[m

[m
       You may have noticed that while some parameters start with the [4moption[24m keyword, some do not.  Parameters start‚Äê[m
       ing with the [4moption[24m keyword correspond to actual DHCP options, while parameters that do  not  start  with  the[m
       option keyword either control the behavior of the DHCP server (e.g., how long a lease dhcpd will give out), or[m
       specify client parameters that are not optional in the DHCP protocol (for example, server-name and filename).[m
[m
       In Figure 1, each host had [4mhost-specific[24m [4mparameters[24m.  These could include such things as the [4mhostname[24m  option,[m
       the  name  of a file to upload (the [4mfilename[24m parameter) and the address of the server from which to upload the[m
       file (the [4mnext-server[24m parameter).  In general, any parameter can appear anywhere that parameters are  allowed,[m
       and will be applied according to the scope in which the parameter appears.[m
[m
       Imagine  that you have a site with a lot of NCD X-Terminals.  These terminals come in a variety of models, and[m
       you want to specify the boot files for each model.  One way to do this would be to have host declarations  for[m
       each server and group them by model:[m
[m
       group {[m
         filename "Xncd19r";[m
         next-server ncd-booter;[m
[m
         host ncd1 { hardware ethernet 0:c0:c3:49:2b:57; }[m
         host ncd4 { hardware ethernet 0:c0:c3:80:fc:32; }[m
         host ncd8 { hardware ethernet 0:c0:c3:22:46:81; }[m
       }[m
[m
       group {[m
         filename "Xncd19c";[m
         next-server ncd-booter;[m
[m
         host ncd2 { hardware ethernet 0:c0:c3:88:2d:81; }[m
         host ncd3 { hardware ethernet 0:c0:c3:00:14:11; }[m
       }[m
[m
       group {[m
         filename "XncdHMX";[m
         next-server ncd-booter;[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 136 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
           [7mmax-lease[27m-time 300;[m
           range 10.0.0.200 10.0.0.253;[m
           allow unknown-clients;[m
         }[m
[m
         # Known clients get this pool.[m
         pool {[m
           option domain-name-servers ns1.example.com, ns2.example.com;[m
           [7mmax-lease[27m-time 28800;[m
           range 10.0.0.5 10.0.0.199;[m
           deny unknown-clients;[m
         }[m
       }[m
[m
       It  is  also possible to set up entirely different subnets for known and unknown clients - address pools exist[m
       at the level of shared networks, so address ranges within pool declarations can be on different subnets.[m
[m
       As you can see in the preceding example, pools can have permit lists that control which  clients  are  allowed[m
       access  to the pool and which aren't.  Each entry in a pool's permit list is introduced with the [4mallow[24m or [4mdeny[24m[m
       keyword.  If a pool has a permit list, then only those clients that match specific entries on the permit  list[m
       will  be  eligible to be assigned addresses from the pool.  If a pool has a deny list, then only those clients[m
       that do not match any entries on the deny list will be eligible.   If both permit and deny lists exist  for  a[m
       pool, then only clients that match the permit list and do not match the deny list will be allowed access.[m
[m
[1mDYNAMIC[0m [1mADDRESS[0m [1mALLOCATION[0m[m
       Address  allocation  is actually only done when a client is in the INIT state and has sent a DHCPDISCOVER mes‚Äê[m
       sage.  If the client thinks it has a valid lease and sends a DHCPREQUEST to initiate or renew that lease,  the[m
       server  has  only  three  choices - it can ignore the DHCPREQUEST, send a DHCPNAK to tell the client it should[m
       stop using the address, or send a DHCPACK, telling the client to go ahead and use the address for a while.[m
[m
       If the server finds the address the client is requesting, and that address is available  to  the  client,  the[m
       server  will send a DHCPACK.  If the address is no longer available, or the client isn't permitted to have it,[m
       the server will send a DHCPNAK.  If the server knows nothing about the address, it will remain silent,  unless[m
       the  address  is  incorrect  for  the  network segment to which the client has been attached and the server is[m
       authoritative for that network segment, in which case the server will send a DHCPNAK even  though  it  doesn't[m
       know about the address.[m
[m

       server  will send a DHCPACK.  If the address is no longer available, or the client isn't permitted to have it,[m
       the server will send a DHCPNAK.  If the server knows nothing about the address, it will remain silent,  unless[m
       the  address  is  incorrect  for  the  network segment to which the client has been attached and the server is[m
       authoritative for that network segment, in which case the server will send a DHCPNAK even  though  it  doesn't[m
       know about the address.[m

[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 194 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       There  may  be  a  host declaration matching the client's identification.  If that host declaration contains a[m
       fixed-address declaration that lists an IP address that is valid for the network segment to which  the  client[m
       is  connected.   In  this  case,  the DHCP server will never do dynamic address allocation.  In this case, the[m
       client is [4mrequired[24m to take the address specified in the host declaration.  If the client sends  a  DHCPREQUEST[m
       for some other address, the server will respond with a DHCPNAK.[m
[m
       When  the DHCP server allocates a new address for a client (remember, this only happens if the client has sent[m
       a DHCPDISCOVER), it first looks to see if the client already has a valid lease on an IP address, or  if  there[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 202 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
          [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-ownership[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmin-balance[0m [4mseconds[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmax-balance[0m [4mseconds[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         This version of the DHCP Server evaluates pool balance on a schedule, rather than on demand  as  leases  are[m
         allocated.   The  latter  approach  proved  to  be slightly klunky when pool misbalanced reach total satura‚Äê[m
         tion...when any server ran out of leases to assign, it also lost its ability to notice it had run dry.[m
[m
         In order to understand pool balance, some elements of its operation first need to be defined.  First,  there[m
         are  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥ leases.  Both of these are referred to as ¬¥free state leases¬¥.  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         are ¬¥the free states¬¥ for the purpose of this document.  The difference is that only the primary  may  allo‚Äê[m
         cate  from  ¬¥free¬¥  leases  unless under special circumstances, and only the secondary may allocate ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         leases.[m
[m
         When pool balance is performed, the only plausible expectation is to provide a 50/50 split of the free state[m
         leases  between  the  two servers.  This is because no one can predict which server will fail, regardless of[m
         the relative load placed upon the two servers, so giving each server half the leases gives both servers  the[m
         same  amount  of ¬¥failure endurance¬¥.  Therefore, there is no way to configure any different behaviour, out‚Äê[m
         side of some very small windows we will describe shortly.[m
[m
         The first thing calculated on any pool balance run is a value referred to as ¬¥lts¬¥,  or  "Leases  To  Send".[m
         This,  simply, is the difference in the count of free and backup leases, divided by two.  For the secondary,[m
         it is the difference in the backup and free leases, divided by two.  The resulting value is signed: if it is[m
         positive, the local server is expected to hand out leases to retain a 50/50 balance.  If it is negative, the[m
         remote server would need to send leases to balance the pool.  Once the lts value reaches zero, the  pool  is[m
         perfectly balanced (give or take one lease in the case of an odd number of total free state leases).[m
[m
         The  current approach is still something of a hybrid of the old approach, marked by the presence of the [1mmax-[0m[m
         [1mlease-misbalance[0m statement.  This parameter configures what used to be a 10% fixed value  in  previous  ver‚Äê[m
         sions: if lts is less than free+backup * [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m percent, then the server will skip balancing a[m
         given pool (it won't bother moving any leases, even if some leases "should" be moved).  The meaning of  this[m
         value  is  also  somewhat  overloaded, however, in that it also governs the estimation of when to attempt to[m
         balance the pool (which may then also be skipped over).  The oldest leases in the free and backup states are[m
         examined.  The time they have resided in their respective queues is used as an estimate to indicate how much[m
         time it is probable it would take before the leases at the top of the list would be consumed (and thus,  how[m
         long  it  would take to use all leases in that state).  This percentage is directly multiplied by this time,[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 528 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         and fit into the schedule if it falls within the [1mmin-balance[0m and [1mmax-balance[0m configured values.  The  sched‚Äê[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 529 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         uled  pool  check time is only moved in a downwards direction, it is never increased.  Lastly, if the lts is[m
         more than double this number in the negative direction,  the  local  server  will  ¬¥panic¬¥  and  transmit  a[m
         Failover protocol POOLREQ message, in the hopes that the remote system will be woken up into action.[m
[m
         Once  the  lts  value  exceeds  the  [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m percentage of total free state leases as described[m
         above, leases are moved to the remote server.  This is done in two passes.[m
[m
         In the first pass, only leases whose most recent bound client would have been served by the remote server  -[m
         according  to the Load Balance Algorithm (see above [1msplit[0m and [1mhba[0m configuration statements) - are given away[m
         to the peer.  This first pass will happily continue to give away leases, decrementing the lts value  by  one[m
         for  each, until the lts value has reached the negative of the total number of leases multiplied by the [1mmax-[0m[m
         [1mlease-ownership[0m percentage.  So it is through this value that you can permit a small misbalance of the lease[m
         pools - for the purpose of giving the peer more than a 50/50 share of leases in the hopes that their clients[m
         might some day return and be allocated by the peer (operating normally).  This process  is  referred  to  as[m
         ¬¥MAC Address Affinity¬¥, but this is somewhat misnamed: it applies equally to DHCP Client Identifier options.[m
         Note also that affinity is applied to leases when they enter the state ¬¥free¬¥ from ¬¥expired¬¥ or  ¬¥released¬¥.[m
         In  this  case also, leases will not be moved from free to backup if the secondary already has more than its[m
         share.[m
[m
         The second pass is only entered into if the first pass fails to reduce the lts underneath the  total  number[m
         of  free state leases multiplied by the [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-ownership[0m percentage.  In this pass, the oldest leases are[m
         given over to the peer without second thought about the Load Balance Algorithm, and this continues until the[m
         lts  falls under this value.  In this way, the local server will also happily keep a small percentage of the[m
         leases that would normally load balance to itself.[m
[m
         So, the [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m value acts as a behavioural gate.  Smaller values will  cause  more  leases  to[m
         transition  states to balance the pools over time, higher values will decrease the amount of change (but may[m
         lead to pool starvation if there's a run on leases).[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 558 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         The [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-ownership[0m value permits a small (percentage) skew in the lease balance of a percentage of  the[m
         total number of free state leases.[m
[m
         Finally, the [1mmin-balance[0m and [1mmax-balance[0m make certain that a scheduled rebalance event happens within a rea‚Äê[m
         sonable timeframe (not to be thrown off by, for example, a 7 year old free lease).[m
[m
         Plausible values for the percentages lie between 0 and 100, inclusive, but values over 50 are indistinguish‚Äê[m
         able from one another (once lts exceeds 50% of the free state leases, one server must therefore have 100% of[m
         the leases in its respective free state).  It is recommended to select a [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-ownership[0m value  that  is[m
         lower  than  the value selected for the [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m value.  [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-ownership[0m defaults to 10, and[m
         [1m[7mmax-lease[27m[0m[1m-misbalance[0m defaults to 15.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 570 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         Plausible values for the [1mmin-balance[0m and [1mmax-balance[0m times also range from 0 to (2^32)-1 (or  the  limit  of[m
         your  local time_t value), but default to values 60 and 3600 respectively (to place balance events between 1[m
         minute and 1 hour).[m
[m
[1mCLIENT[0m [1mCLASSING[0m[m
       Clients can be separated into classes, and treated differently depending on what class they are in.  This sep‚Äê[m
       aration  can  be done either with a conditional statement, or with a match statement within the class declara‚Äê[m
       tion.  It is possible to specify a limit on the total number of clients within a particular class or  subclass[m
       that may hold leases at one time, and it is possible to specify automatic subclassing based on the contents of[m
       the client packet.[m
[m
       To add clients to classes based on conditional evaluation, you can specify a matching expression in the  class[m
       statement:[m
[m
       class "ras-clients" {[m
         match if substring (option dhcp-client-identifier, 1, 3) = "RAS";[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 586 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       }[m
[m
       Note  that  whether  you  use  matching  expressions or add statements (or both) to classify clients, you must[m
       always write a class declaration for any class that you use.  If there will be no match statement and  no  in-[m
       scope statements for a class, the declaration should look like this:[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 591 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[m
       class "ras-clients" {[m
       }[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 595 (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[1mSUBCLASSES[0m[m
       In  addition  to classes, it is possible to declare subclasses.  A subclass is a class with the same name as a[m
       regular class, but with a specific submatch expression which is hashed for quick  matching.   This  is  essen‚Äê[m
       tially  a speed hack - the main difference between five classes with match expressions and one class with five[m
       subclasses is that it will be quicker to find the subclasses.  Subclasses work as follows:[m
[m
       class "allocation-class-1" {[m
         match pick-first-value (option dhcp-client-identifier, hardware);[m

       a smaller set of addresses, possibly with short [7mlease[27m times, that are available for unknown clients.   If  you[m

           option domain-name-servers bogus.example.com;[m
           max-[7mlease[27m-time 300;[m
           range 10.0.0.200 10.0.0.253;[m
           allow unknown-clients;[m
         }[m
[m
         # Known clients get this pool.[m
         pool {[m
           option domain-name-servers ns1.example.com, ns2.example.com;[m
           max-[7mlease[27m-time 28800;[m
           range 10.0.0.5 10.0.0.199;[m
           deny unknown-clients;[m
         }[m
       }[m
[m
       It  is  also possible to set up entirely different subnets for known and unknown clients - address pools exist[m
       at the level of shared networks, so address ranges within pool declarations can be on different subnets.[m
[m
       As you can see in the preceding example, pools can have permit lists that control which  clients  are  allowed[m
       access  to the pool and which aren't.  Each entry in a pool's permit list is introduced with the [4mallow[24m or [4mdeny[24m[m
       keyword.  If a pool has a permit list, then only those clients that match specific entries on the permit  list[m
       will  be  eligible to be assigned addresses from the pool.  If a pool has a deny list, then only those clients[m
       that do not match any entries on the deny list will be eligible.   If both permit and deny lists exist  for  a[m
       pool, then only clients that match the permit list and do not match the deny list will be allowed access.[m
[m
[1mDYNAMIC[0m [1mADDRESS[0m [1mALLOCATION[0m[m
       Address  allocation  is actually only done when a client is in the INIT state and has sent a DHCPDISCOVER mes‚Äê[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 183/1945 10% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       sage.  If the client thinks it has a valid [7mlease[27m and sends a DHCPREQUEST to initiate or renew that [7mlease[27m,  the[m
       server  has  only  three  choices - it can ignore the DHCPREQUEST, send a DHCPNAK to tell the client it should[m
       stop using the address, or send a DHCPACK, telling the client to go ahead and use the address for a while.[m
[m
       If the server finds the address the client is requesting, and that address is available  to  the  client,  the[m
       server  will send a DHCPACK.  If the address is no longer available, or the client isn't permitted to have it,[m
       the server will send a DHCPNAK.  If the server knows nothing about the address, it will remain silent,  unless[m
       the  address  is  incorrect  for  the  network segment to which the client has been attached and the server is[m
       authoritative for that network segment, in which case the server will send a DHCPNAK even  though  it  doesn't[m
       know about the address.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 194/1945 11% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       There  may  be  a  host declaration matching the client's identification.  If that host declaration contains a[m
       fixed-address declaration that lists an IP address that is valid for the network segment to which  the  client[m
       is  connected.   In  this  case,  the DHCP server will never do dynamic address allocation.  In this case, the[m
       client is [4mrequired[24m to take the address specified in the host declaration.  If the client sends  a  DHCPREQUEST[m
       for some other address, the server will respond with a DHCPNAK.[m
[m
       When  the DHCP server allocates a new address for a client (remember, this only happens if the client has sent[m
       a DHCPDISCOVER), it first looks to see if the client already has a valid [7mlease[27m on an IP address, or  if  there[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 202/1945 12% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       is  an  old  IP  address the client had before that hasn't yet been reassigned.  In that case, the server will[m
       take that address and check it to see if the client is still permitted to use it.  If the client is no  longer[m
       permitted  to  use it, the [7mlease[27m is freed if the server thought it was still in use - the fact that the client[m
       has sent a DHCPDISCOVER proves to the server that the client is no longer using the [7mlease[27m.[m
[m
       If no existing [7mlease[27m is found, or if the client is forbidden to receive the existing [7mlease[27m,  then  the  server[m
       will  look  in  the  list of address pools for the network segment to which the client is attached for a [7mlease[27m[m
       that is not in use and that the client is permitted to have.   It  looks  through  each  pool  declaration  in[m
       sequence  (all [4mrange[24m declarations that appear outside of pool declarations are grouped into a single pool with[m
       no permit list).  If the permit list for the pool allows the client to be allocated an address from that pool,[m
       the  pool is examined to see if there is an address available.  If so, then the client is tentatively assigned[m
       that address.  Otherwise, the next pool is tested.  If no addresses are found that  can  be  assigned  to  the[m
       client, no response is sent to the client.[m
[m
       If  an  address  is found that the client is permitted to have, and that has never been assigned to any client[m
       before, the address is immediately allocated to the client.  If the address is available  for  allocation  but[m
       has  been  previously  assigned  to  a  different  client, the server will keep looking in hopes of finding an[m
       address that has never before been assigned to a client.[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 220/1945 13% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[m
       The DHCP server generates the list of available IP addresses from a hash table.  This means that the addresses[m
       are  not  sorted  in  any  particular  order, and so it is not possible to predict the order in which the DHCP[m
       server will allocate IP addresses.  Users of previous versions of the ISC DHCP server may have  become  accus‚Äê[m
       tomed to the DHCP server allocating IP addresses in ascending order, but this is no longer possible, and there[m
       is no way to configure this behavior with version 3 of the ISC DHCP server.[m
[m
[1mIP[0m [1mADDRESS[0m [1mCONFLICT[0m [1mPREVENTION[0m[m
       The DHCP server checks IP addresses to see if they are in use before allocating them to clients.  It does this[m
       by  sending an ICMP Echo request message to the IP address being allocated.  If no ICMP Echo reply is received[m
       within a second, the address is assumed to be free.  This is only done for [7mlease[27ms that have been specified  in[m
       range  statements, and only when the [7mlease[27m is thought by the DHCP server to be free - i.e., the DHCP server or[m
       its failover peer has not listed the [7mlease[27m as in use.[m
[m
       If a response is received to an ICMP Echo request, the DHCP server assumes that there is a configuration error[m
       -  the  IP  address  is in use by some host on the network that is not a DHCP client.  It marks the address as[m
       abandoned, and will not assign it to clients.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 238/1945 14% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       If a DHCP client tries to get an IP address, but none are available, but there  are  abandoned  IP  addresses,[m
       then  the  DHCP  server will attempt to reclaim an abandoned IP address.  It marks one IP address as free, and[m
       then does the same ICMP Echo request check described previously.  If there is  no  answer  to  the  ICMP  Echo[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 241/1945 14% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       request, the address is assigned to the client.[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 242/1945 14% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[m
       The  DHCP  server does not cycle through abandoned IP addresses if the first IP address it tries to reclaim is[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 244/1945 14% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       free.  Rather, when the next DHCPDISCOVER comes in from the client, it will attempt a new allocation using the[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 245/1945 14% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       same method described here, and will typically try a new IP address.[m
[m
[1mDHCP[0m [1mFAILOVER[0m[m
       This  version  of  the  ISC  DHCP  server supports the DHCP failover protocol as documented in draft-ietf-dhc-[m
       failover-12.txt.  This is not a final protocol document, and we have not done  interoperability  testing  with[m
       other  vendors'  implementations of this protocol, so you must not assume that this implementation conforms to[m
       the standard.  If you wish to use the failover protocol, make sure that both failover peers  are  running  the[m
       same version of the ISC DHCP server.[m
[m
       The  failover  protocol  allows  two DHCP servers (and no more than two) to share a common address pool.  Each[m
       server will have about half of the available IP addresses in the pool at any given time  for  allocation.   If[m
       one  server  fails,  the  other  server  will  continue to renew [7mlease[27ms out of the pool, and will allocate new[m
       addresses out of the roughly half of available addresses that it had when communications with the other server[m
       were lost.[m
[m
       It is possible during a prolonged failure to tell the remaining server that the other server is down, in which[m
       case the remaining server will (over time) reclaim all the addresses the other server had available for  allo‚Äê[m
       cation, and begin to reuse them.  This is called putting the server into the PARTNER-DOWN state.[m
[m
       You  can put the server into the PARTNER-DOWN state either by using the [1momshell[0m [1m(1)[0m command or by stopping the[m
       server, editing the last failover state declaration in the [7mlease[27m file, and restarting the server.  If you  use[m
       this last method, change the "my state" line to:[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 267/1945 16% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 268/1945 16% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       [1mfailover[0m [1mpeer[0m [4mname[24m [1mstate[0m [1m{[0m[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 269/1945 16% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[Kss[Kee[Kcc[Koo[Knn[Kdd[K[1;1H       its failover peer has not listed the lease as in use.[m
[2;1H[m
[3;1H       If a response is received to an ICMP Echo request, the DHCP server assumes that there is a configuration error[m
[4;1H       -  the  IP  address  is in use by some host on the network that is not a DHCP client.  It marks the address as[m
[5;1H       abandoned, and will not assign it to clients.[m
[6;1H[m
[7;1H       If a DHCP client tries to get an IP address, but none are available, but there  are  abandoned  IP  addresses,[m
[8;1H       then  the  DHCP  server will attempt to reclaim an abandoned IP address.  It marks one IP address as free, and[m
[9;1H       then does the same ICMP Echo request check described previously.  If there is  no  answer  to  the  ICMP  Echo[m
[10;1H       request, the address is assigned to the client.[m
[11;1H[m
[12;1H       The  DHCP  server does not cycle through abandoned IP addresses if the first IP address it tries to reclaim is[m
[13;1H       free.  Rather, when the next DHCPDISCOVER comes in from the client, it will attempt a new allocation using the[m
[14;1H       same method described here, and will typically try a new IP address.[m
[15;1H[m

[16;1H[1mDHCP[0m [1mFAILOVER[0m[m

[32;1H[m
[33;1H       You  can put the server into the PARTNER-DOWN state either by using the [1momshell[0m [1m(1)[0m command or by stopping the[m

[36;1H[m
[37;1H       [1mfailover[0m [1mpeer[0m [4mname[24m [1mstate[0m [1m{[0m[m
[38;1H...skipping...
       The  failover  protocol defines a primary server role and a [7msecond[27mary server role.  There are some differences[m
       in how primaries and [7msecond[27maries act, but most of the differences simply have to do with providing a  way  for[m
       each  peer to behave in the opposite way from the other.  So one server must be configured as primary, and the[m
       other must be configured as [7msecond[27mary, and it doesn't matter too much which one is which.[m
[m
[1mFAILOVER[0m [1mSTARTUP[0m[m
       When a server starts that has not previously communicated with its failover peer, it must establish communica‚Äê[m
       tions  with  its  failover  peer  and synchronize with it before it can serve clients.  This can happen either[m
       because you have just configured your DHCP servers to perform failover for the first time, or because  one  of[m
       your failover servers has failed catastrophically and lost its database.[m
[m
       The  initial  recovery  process  is designed to ensure that when one failover peer loses its database and then[m
       resynchronizes, any leases that the failed server gave out before it failed will be honored.  When the  failed[m
       server starts up, it notices that it has no saved failover state, and attempts to contact its peer.[m
[m
       When  it  has  established  contact, it asks the peer for a complete copy its peer's lease database.  The peer[m
       then sends its complete database, and sends a message indicating that it is  done.   The  failed  server  then[m
       waits until MCLT has passed, and once MCLT has passed both servers make the transition back into normal opera‚Äê[m
       tion.  This waiting period ensures that any leases the failed server may have given out while out  of  contact[m
       with its partner will have expired.[m
[m
       While  the  failed server is recovering, its partner remains in the partner-down state, which means that it is[m
       serving all clients.  The failed server provides no service at all to DHCP clients until it has made the tran‚Äê[m
       sition into normal operation.[m
[m
       In  the  case where both servers detect that they have never before communicated with their partner, they both[m
       come up in this recovery state and follow the procedure we have just described.  In this case, no service will[m
       be provided to DHCP clients until MCLT has expired.[m
[m
[1mCONFIGURING[0m [1mFAILOVER[0m[m
       In  order  to  configure failover, you need to write a peer declaration that configures the failover protocol,[m
       and you need to write peer references in each pool declaration for which you want to do failover.  You do  not[m
       have  to  do  failover  for  all  pools  on a given network segment.   You must not tell one server it's doing[m
       failover on a particular address pool and tell the other it is not.  You must  not  have  any  common  address[m
       pools on which you are not doing failover.  A pool declaration that utilizes failover would look like this:[m
[m
       pool {[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 322/1945 19% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K            failover peer "foo";[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 323/1945 19% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K            [4mpool[24m [4mspecific[24m [4mparameters[24m[m
       };[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 325/1945 19% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
         load balance max [7msecond[27ms 3;[m
       }[m
[m
       include "/etc/dhcpd.master";[m

[4;1H[m
[5;1H       The [4maddress[24m statement[m
[6;1H[m
[7;1H         [1maddress[0m [4maddress[24m[1m;[0m[m
[8;1H[m
[9;1H         The  [1maddress[0m statement declares the IP address or DNS name on which the server should listen for connections[m
[10;1H         from its failover peer, and also the value to use for the DHCP Failover Protocol server identifier.  Because[m
[11;1H         this value is used as an identifier, it may not be omitted.[m
[12;1H[m
[13;1H       The [4mpeer[24m [4maddress[24m statement[m
[14;1H[m
[15;1H         [1mpeer[0m [1maddress[0m [4maddress[24m[1m;[0m[m
[16;1H[m
[17;1H         The  [1mpeer[0m  [1maddress[0m statement declares the IP address or DNS name to which the server should connect to reach[m
[18;1H         its failover peer for failover messages.[m
[19;1H[m
[20;1H       The [4mport[24m statement[m
[21;1H[m
[22;1H         [1mport[0m [4mport-number[24m[1m;[0m[m
[23;1H[m
[24;1H         The [1mport[0m statement declares the TCP port on which the server should listen for connections from its failover[m
[25;1H         peer.   This  statement  may  be  omitted,  in  which case the IANA assigned port number 647 will be used by[m
[26;1H         default.[m
[27;1H[m
[28;1H       The [4mpeer[24m [4mport[24m statement[m
[29;1H[m
[30;1H         [1mpeer[0m [1mport[0m [4mport-number[24m[1m;[0m[m
[31;1H[m
[32;1H         The [1mpeer[0m [1mport[0m statement declares the TCP port to which the server should connect to reach its failover  peer[m
[33;1H         for  failover messages.  This statement may be omitted, in which case the IANA assigned port number 647 will[m
[34;1H         be used by default.[m
[35;1H[m
[36;1H       The [4mmax-response-delay[24m statement[m
[37;1H[m
[38;1H         [1mmax-response-delay[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmax-response-delay[0m statement tells the DHCP server how many [7mseconds[27m may pass without receiving a message[m
         from  its  failover  peer  before it assumes that connection has failed.  This number should be small enough[m
         that a transient network failure that breaks the connection will not result in the servers being out of com‚Äê[m
         munication  for  a  long time, but large enough that the server isn't constantly making and breaking connec‚Äê[m
         tions.  This parameter must be specified.[m
[m
       The [4mmax-unacked-updates[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mmax-unacked-updates[0m [4mcount[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmax-unacked-updates[0m statement tells the remote DHCP server how many BNDUPD messages it can  send  before[m
         it  receives a BNDACK from the local system.  We don't have enough operational experience to say what a good[m
         value for this is, but 10 seems to work.  This parameter must be specified.[m
[m
       The [4mmclt[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mmclt[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmclt[0m statement defines the Maximum Client Lead Time.  It must be specified on the primary, and  may  not[m
         be  specified  on  the  secondary.   This  is  the length of time for which a lease may be renewed by either[m
         failover peer without contacting the other.  The longer you set this, the longer it will take for  the  run‚Äê[m
         ning  server to recover IP addresses after moving into PARTNER-DOWN state.  The shorter you set it, the more[m
         load your servers will experience when they are not communicating.  A value of something like 3600 is proba‚Äê[m
         bly reasonable, but again bear in mind that we have no real operational experience with this.[m
[m
       The [4msplit[24m statement[m
[m
         [1msplit[0m [4mindex[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The  split  statement specifies the split between the primary and secondary for the purposes of load balanc‚Äê[m
         ing.  Whenever a client makes a DHCP request, the DHCP server runs a  hash  on  the  client  identification,[m
         resulting  in value from 0 to 255.  This is used as an index into a 256 bit field.  If the bit at that index[m
         is set, the primary is responsible.  If the bit at that index is not set, the secondary is responsible.  The[m
         [1msplit[0m  value  determines  how many of the leading bits are set to one.  So, in practice, higher split values[m
         will cause the primary to serve more clients than the secondary.  Lower split values, the  converse.   Legal[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 429/1945 23% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         values are between 0 and 255, of which the most reasonable is 128.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 431/1945 23% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       The [4mhba[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mhba[0m [4mcolon-separated-hex-list[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The  hba  statement  specifies the split between the primary and secondary as a bitmap rather than a cutoff,[m
         which theoretically allows for finer-grained control.  In practice, there is probably no need for such fine-[m
         grained control, however.  An example hba statement:[m
[m
           hba ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:[m
               00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00;[m
[m
         This is equivalent to a [1msplit[0m [1m128;[0m statement, and identical.  The following two examples are also equivalent[m
         to a [1msplit[0m of 128, but are not identical:[m
[m
           hba aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:[m
               aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa;[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 447/1945 24% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
       The [4mload[24m [4mbalance[24m [4mmax[24m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mload[0m [1mbalance[0m [1mmax[0m [1m[7mseconds[27m[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         This statement allows you to configure a cutoff after which load balancing is disabled.  The cutoff is based[m
         on the number of [7mseconds[27m since the client sent its first DHCPDISCOVER or DHCPREQUEST message, and only works[m
         with clients that correctly implement the [4msecs[24m field - fortunately most clients do.   We  recommend  setting[m
         this  to  something  like 3 or 5.  The effect of this is that if one of the failover peers gets into a state[m
         where it is responding to failover messages but not responding to some client requests, the  other  failover[m
         peer will take over its client load automatically as the clients retry.[m
[m
       The [4mauto-partner-down[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mauto-partner-down[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         This  statement  instructs the server to initiate a timed delay upon entering the communications-interrupted[m
         state (any situation of being out-of-contact with the remote failover  peer).   At  the  conclusion  of  the[m
         timer,  the  server  will  automatically  enter the partner-down state.  This permits the server to allocate[m
         leases from the partner's free lease pool after an STOS+MCLT timer expires, which can be  dangerous  if  the[m
         partner is in fact operating at the time (the two servers will give conflicting bindings).[m
[m
         Think  very  carefully before enabling this feature.  The partner-down and communications-interrupted states[m
         are intentionally segregated because there do exist situations where a failover server can fail to  communi‚Äê[m
         cate  with  its peer, but still has the ability to receive and reply to requests from DHCP clients.  In gen‚Äê[m
         eral, this feature should only be used in those deployments where the failover  servers  are  directly  con‚Äê[m
         nected to one another, such as by a dedicated hardwired link ("a heartbeat cable").[m
[m
         A zero value disables the auto-partner-down feature (also the default), and any positive value indicates the[m
         time in [7mseconds[27m to wait before automatically entering partner-down.[m
[m
       The Failover pool balance statements.[m
[m
          [1mmax-lease-misbalance[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmax-lease-ownership[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmin-balance[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmax-balance[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 496/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         This version of the DHCP Server evaluates pool balance on a schedule, rather than on demand  as  leases  are[m
         allocated.   The  latter  approach  proved  to  be slightly klunky when pool misbalanced reach total satura‚Äê[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 498/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         tion...when any server ran out of leases to assign, it also lost its ability to notice it had run dry.[m
[m
         In order to understand pool balance, some elements of its operation first need to be defined.  First,  there[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 501/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         are  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥ leases.  Both of these are referred to as ¬¥free state leases¬¥.  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         are ¬¥the free states¬¥ for the purpose of this document.  The difference is that only the primary  may  allo‚Äê[m
         cate  from  ¬¥free¬¥  leases  unless under special circumstances, and only the secondary may allocate ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         leases.[m
[m
         When pool balance is performed, the only plausible expectation is to provide a 50/50 split of the free state[m
         leases  between  the  two servers.  This is because no one can predict which server will fail, regardless of[m
         the relative load placed upon the two servers, so giving each server half the leases gives both servers  the[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 509/1945 28% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         same  amount  of ¬¥failure endurance¬¥.  Therefore, there is no way to configure any different behaviour, out‚Äê[m
         side of some very small windows we will describe shortly.[m
[m
         The first thing calculated on any pool balance run is a value referred to as ¬¥lts¬¥,  or  "Leases  To  Send".[m
         This,  simply, is the difference in the count of free and backup leases, divided by two.  For the secondary,[m
         it is the difference in the backup and free leases, divided by two.  The resulting value is signed: if it is[m
         positive, the local server is expected to hand out leases to retain a 50/50 balance.  If it is negative, the[m
         remote server would need to send leases to balance the pool.  Once the lts value reaches zero, the  pool  is[m
         perfectly balanced (give or take one lease in the case of an odd number of total free state leases).[m
[m
         The  current approach is still something of a hybrid of the old approach, marked by the presence of the [1mmax-[0m[m
         [1mlease-misbalance[0m statement.  This parameter configures what used to be a 10% fixed value  in  previous  ver‚Äê[m
         sions: if lts is less than free+backup * [1mmax-lease-misbalance[0m percent, then the server will skip balancing a[m
         given pool (it won't bother moving any leases, even if some leases "should" be moved).  The meaning of  this[m
         value  is  also  somewhat  overloaded, however, in that it also governs the estimation of when to attempt to[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 524/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         balance the pool (which may then also be skipped over).  The oldest leases in the free and backup states are[m
         examined.  The time they have resided in their respective queues is used as an estimate to indicate how much[m
         time it is probable it would take before the leases at the top of the list would be consumed (and thus,  how[m
         long  it  would take to use all leases in that state).  This percentage is directly multiplied by this time,[m
         and fit into the schedule if it falls within the [1mmin-balance[0m and [1mmax-balance[0m configured values.  The  sched‚Äê[m
         uled  pool  check time is only moved in a downwards direction, it is never increased.  Lastly, if the lts is[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 530/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         more than double this number in the negative direction,  the  local  server  will  ¬¥panic¬¥  and  transmit  a[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 531/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
       UTC  time  string  e.g.   4 2007/08/24 09:14:32 or a string with time zone offset in [7mseconds[27m e.g. 4 2007/08/24[m
       11:14:32 -7200[m

[1mREFERENCE:[0m [1mPARAMETERS[0m[m
       The [4madaptive-lease-time-threshold[24m statement[m
[m
         [1madaptive-lease-time-threshold[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         When the number of allocated leases within a pool rises above the [4mpercentage[24m given in  this  statement,  the[m
         DHCP  server  decreases the lease length for new clients within this pool to [4mmin-lease-time[24m [7mseconds[27m. Clients[m
         renewing an already valid (long) leases get at least the remaining time from the current  lease.  Since  the[m
         leases  expire  faster,  the server may either recover more quickly or avoid pool exhaustion entirely.  Once[m
         the number of allocated leases drop below the threshold, the server reverts  back  to  normal  lease  times.[m
         Valid percentages are between 1 and 99.[m
[m
       The [4malways-broadcast[24m statement[m
[m
         [1malways-broadcast[0m [4mflag[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The DHCP and BOOTP protocols both require DHCP and BOOTP clients to set the broadcast bit in the flags field[m
         of the BOOTP message header.  Unfortunately, some DHCP and BOOTP clients do not do this, and  therefore  may[m
         not  receive  responses from the DHCP server.  The DHCP server can be made to always broadcast its responses[m
         to clients by setting this flag to ¬¥on¬¥ for the relevant scope; relevant scopes would  be  inside  a  condi‚Äê[m
         tional  statement,  as a parameter for a class, or as a parameter for a host declaration.  To avoid creating[m
         excess broadcast traffic on your network, we recommend that you restrict the use of this option  to  as  few[m
         clients  as  possible.  For example, the Microsoft DHCP client is known not to have this problem, as are the[m
         OpenTransport and ISC DHCP clients.[m
[m
       The [4malways-reply-rfc1048[24m statement[m
[m
         [1malways-reply-rfc1048[0m [4mflag[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         Some BOOTP clients expect RFC1048-style responses, but do not follow RFC1048 when  sending  their  requests.[m
         You  can  tell that a client is having this problem if it is not getting the options you have configured for[m
         it and if you see in the server log the message  "(non-rfc1048)"  printed  with  each  BOOTREQUEST  that  is[m
         logged.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 1269/1945 67% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         If  you  want  to send rfc1048 options to such a client, you can set the [1malways-reply-rfc1048[0m option in that[m
         client's host declaration, and the DHCP server will respond with an  RFC-1048-style  vendor  options  field.[m
         This flag can be set in any scope, and will affect all clients covered by that scope.[m
[m
       The [4mauthoritative[24m statement[m

         [1mauthoritative;[0m[m

         [1mnot[0m [1mauthoritative;[0m[m

         UTC, while the [4mlocal[24m format prints the system [7mseconds[27m-since-epoch, and helpfully provides the day  and  time[m
         in  the  system timezone in a comment.  The time formats are described in detail in the dhcpd.leases(5) man‚Äê[m
         page.[m

       The [4mddns-hostname[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mddns-hostname[0m [4mname[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [4mname[24m parameter should be the hostname that will be used in setting up the client's A  and  PTR  records.[m
         If  no ddns-hostname is specified in scope, then the server will derive the hostname automatically, using an[m
         algorithm that varies for each of the different update methods.[m

       The [4mddns-domainname[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mddns-domainname[0m [4mname[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m

         qualified domain-name (FQDN).[m

       The [4mddns-rev-domainname[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mddns-rev-domainname[0m [4mname[24m[1m;[0m The [4mname[24m parameter should be the domain name that will be appended to the client's[m
         reversed IP address to produce a name for use in  the  client's  PTR  record.   By  default,  this  is  "in-[m
         addr.arpa.", but the default can be overridden here.[m
[m
         The  reversed  IP  address  to which this domain name is appended is always the IP address of the client, in[m
         dotted quad notation, reversed - for example, if the IP address assigned to the client is 10.17.92.74,  then[m
         the  reversed IP address is 74.92.17.10.  So a client with that IP address would, by default, be given a PTR[m
         record of 10.17.92.74.in-addr.arpa.[m

       The statements in the peer declaration are as follows:[m
[m
       The [4mprimary[24m and [4msecondary[24m statements[m
[m
         [ [1mprimary[0m | [1msecondary[0m ][1m;[0m[m
[m
         This determines whether the server is primary or secondary, as described earlier under DHCP FAILOVER.[m
[m
       The [4maddress[24m statement[m
[m
         [1maddress[0m [4maddress[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The  [1maddress[0m statement declares the IP address or DNS name on which the server should listen for connections[m
         from its failover peer, and also the value to use for the DHCP Failover Protocol server identifier.  Because[m
         this value is used as an identifier, it may not be omitted.[m

       The [4mpeer[24m [4maddress[24m statement[m

         its failover peer for failover messages.[m
[m
       The [4mport[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mport[0m [4mport-number[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mport[0m statement declares the TCP port on which the server should listen for connections from its failover[m
         peer.   This  statement  may  be  omitted,  in  which case the IANA assigned port number 647 will be used by[m
         default.[m
[m
       The [4mpeer[24m [4mport[24m statement[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 383/1945 21% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
         [1mmax-response-delay[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmax-response-delay[0m statement tells the DHCP server how many [7mseconds[27m may pass without receiving a message[m
         from  its  failover  peer  before it assumes that connection has failed.  This number should be small enough[m
         that a transient network failure that breaks the connection will not result in the servers being out of com‚Äê[m
         munication  for  a  long time, but large enough that the server isn't constantly making and breaking connec‚Äê[m
         tions.  This parameter must be specified.[m
[m
       The [4mmax-unacked-updates[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mmax-unacked-updates[0m [4mcount[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmax-unacked-updates[0m statement tells the remote DHCP server how many BNDUPD messages it can  send  before[m
         it  receives a BNDACK from the local system.  We don't have enough operational experience to say what a good[m
         value for this is, but 10 seems to work.  This parameter must be specified.[m
[m
       The [4mmclt[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mmclt[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [1mmclt[0m statement defines the Maximum Client Lead Time.  It must be specified on the primary, and  may  not[m
         be  specified  on  the  secondary.   This  is  the length of time for which a lease may be renewed by either[m
         failover peer without contacting the other.  The longer you set this, the longer it will take for  the  run‚Äê[m
         ning  server to recover IP addresses after moving into PARTNER-DOWN state.  The shorter you set it, the more[m
         load your servers will experience when they are not communicating.  A value of something like 3600 is proba‚Äê[m
         bly reasonable, but again bear in mind that we have no real operational experience with this.[m
[m
       The [4msplit[24m statement[m
[m
         [1msplit[0m [4mindex[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The  split  statement specifies the split between the primary and secondary for the purposes of load balanc‚Äê[m
         ing.  Whenever a client makes a DHCP request, the DHCP server runs a  hash  on  the  client  identification,[m
         resulting  in value from 0 to 255.  This is used as an index into a 256 bit field.  If the bit at that index[m
         is set, the primary is responsible.  If the bit at that index is not set, the secondary is responsible.  The[m
         [1msplit[0m  value  determines  how many of the leading bits are set to one.  So, in practice, higher split values[m
         will cause the primary to serve more clients than the secondary.  Lower split values, the  converse.   Legal[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 429/1945 23% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         values are between 0 and 255, of which the most reasonable is 128.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 431/1945 23% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K       The [4mhba[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mhba[0m [4mcolon-separated-hex-list[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The  hba  statement  specifies the split between the primary and secondary as a bitmap rather than a cutoff,[m
         which theoretically allows for finer-grained control.  In practice, there is probably no need for such fine-[m
         grained control, however.  An example hba statement:[m
[m
           hba ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:[m
               00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00:00;[m
[m
         This is equivalent to a [1msplit[0m [1m128;[0m statement, and identical.  The following two examples are also equivalent[m
         to a [1msplit[0m of 128, but are not identical:[m
[m
           hba aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:[m
               aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa:aa;[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 447/1945 24% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
       The [4mload[24m [4mbalance[24m [4mmax[24m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mload[0m [1mbalance[0m [1mmax[0m [1m[7mseconds[27m[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         This statement allows you to configure a cutoff after which load balancing is disabled.  The cutoff is based[m
         on the number of [7mseconds[27m since the client sent its first DHCPDISCOVER or DHCPREQUEST message, and only works[m
         with clients that correctly implement the [4msecs[24m field - fortunately most clients do.   We  recommend  setting[m
         this  to  something  like 3 or 5.  The effect of this is that if one of the failover peers gets into a state[m
         where it is responding to failover messages but not responding to some client requests, the  other  failover[m
         peer will take over its client load automatically as the clients retry.[m
[m
       The [4mauto-partner-down[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mauto-partner-down[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         This  statement  instructs the server to initiate a timed delay upon entering the communications-interrupted[m
         state (any situation of being out-of-contact with the remote failover  peer).   At  the  conclusion  of  the[m
         timer,  the  server  will  automatically  enter the partner-down state.  This permits the server to allocate[m
         leases from the partner's free lease pool after an STOS+MCLT timer expires, which can be  dangerous  if  the[m
         partner is in fact operating at the time (the two servers will give conflicting bindings).[m
[m
         Think  very  carefully before enabling this feature.  The partner-down and communications-interrupted states[m
         are intentionally segregated because there do exist situations where a failover server can fail to  communi‚Äê[m
         cate  with  its peer, but still has the ability to receive and reply to requests from DHCP clients.  In gen‚Äê[m
         eral, this feature should only be used in those deployments where the failover  servers  are  directly  con‚Äê[m
         nected to one another, such as by a dedicated hardwired link ("a heartbeat cable").[m
[m
         A zero value disables the auto-partner-down feature (also the default), and any positive value indicates the[m
         time in [7mseconds[27m to wait before automatically entering partner-down.[m
[m
       The Failover pool balance statements.[m
[m
          [1mmax-lease-misbalance[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmax-lease-ownership[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmin-balance[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
          [1mmax-balance[0m [4m[7mseconds[27m[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 496/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         This version of the DHCP Server evaluates pool balance on a schedule, rather than on demand  as  leases  are[m
         allocated.   The  latter  approach  proved  to  be slightly klunky when pool misbalanced reach total satura‚Äê[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 498/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         tion...when any server ran out of leases to assign, it also lost its ability to notice it had run dry.[m
[m
         In order to understand pool balance, some elements of its operation first need to be defined.  First,  there[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 501/1945 27% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         are  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥ leases.  Both of these are referred to as ¬¥free state leases¬¥.  ¬¥free¬¥ and ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         are ¬¥the free states¬¥ for the purpose of this document.  The difference is that only the primary  may  allo‚Äê[m
         cate  from  ¬¥free¬¥  leases  unless under special circumstances, and only the secondary may allocate ¬¥backup¬¥[m
         leases.[m
[m
         When pool balance is performed, the only plausible expectation is to provide a 50/50 split of the free state[m
         leases  between  the  two servers.  This is because no one can predict which server will fail, regardless of[m
         the relative load placed upon the two servers, so giving each server half the leases gives both servers  the[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 509/1945 28% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         same  amount  of ¬¥failure endurance¬¥.  Therefore, there is no way to configure any different behaviour, out‚Äê[m
         side of some very small windows we will describe shortly.[m
[m
         The first thing calculated on any pool balance run is a value referred to as ¬¥lts¬¥,  or  "Leases  To  Send".[m
         This,  simply, is the difference in the count of free and backup leases, divided by two.  For the secondary,[m
         it is the difference in the backup and free leases, divided by two.  The resulting value is signed: if it is[m
         positive, the local server is expected to hand out leases to retain a 50/50 balance.  If it is negative, the[m
         remote server would need to send leases to balance the pool.  Once the lts value reaches zero, the  pool  is[m
         perfectly balanced (give or take one lease in the case of an odd number of total free state leases).[m
[m
         The  current approach is still something of a hybrid of the old approach, marked by the presence of the [1mmax-[0m[m
         [1mlease-misbalance[0m statement.  This parameter configures what used to be a 10% fixed value  in  previous  ver‚Äê[m
         sions: if lts is less than free+backup * [1mmax-lease-misbalance[0m percent, then the server will skip balancing a[m
         given pool (it won't bother moving any leases, even if some leases "should" be moved).  The meaning of  this[m
         value  is  also  somewhat  overloaded, however, in that it also governs the estimation of when to attempt to[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 524/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         balance the pool (which may then also be skipped over).  The oldest leases in the free and backup states are[m
         examined.  The time they have resided in their respective queues is used as an estimate to indicate how much[m
         time it is probable it would take before the leases at the top of the list would be consumed (and thus,  how[m
         long  it  would take to use all leases in that state).  This percentage is directly multiplied by this time,[m
         and fit into the schedule if it falls within the [1mmin-balance[0m and [1mmax-balance[0m configured values.  The  sched‚Äê[m
         uled  pool  check time is only moved in a downwards direction, it is never increased.  Lastly, if the lts is[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 530/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         more than double this number in the negative direction,  the  local  server  will  ¬¥panic¬¥  and  transmit  a[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 531/1945 29% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K...skipping...
       UTC  time  string  e.g.   4 2007/08/24 09:14:32 or a string with time zone offset in [7mseconds[27m e.g. 4 2007/08/24[m
       11:14:32 -7200[m
[m
[1mREFERENCE:[0m [1mPARAMETERS[0m[m
       The [4madaptive-lease-time-threshold[24m statement[m
[m
         [1madaptive-lease-time-threshold[0m [4mpercentage[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         When the number of allocated leases within a pool rises above the [4mpercentage[24m given in  this  statement,  the[m
         DHCP  server  decreases the lease length for new clients within this pool to [4mmin-lease-time[24m [7mseconds[27m. Clients[m
         renewing an already valid (long) leases get at least the remaining time from the current  lease.  Since  the[m
         leases  expire  faster,  the server may either recover more quickly or avoid pool exhaustion entirely.  Once[m
         the number of allocated leases drop below the threshold, the server reverts  back  to  normal  lease  times.[m
         Valid percentages are between 1 and 99.[m
[m
       The [4malways-broadcast[24m statement[m
[m
         [1malways-broadcast[0m [4mflag[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The DHCP and BOOTP protocols both require DHCP and BOOTP clients to set the broadcast bit in the flags field[m
         of the BOOTP message header.  Unfortunately, some DHCP and BOOTP clients do not do this, and  therefore  may[m
         not  receive  responses from the DHCP server.  The DHCP server can be made to always broadcast its responses[m
         to clients by setting this flag to ¬¥on¬¥ for the relevant scope; relevant scopes would  be  inside  a  condi‚Äê[m
         tional  statement,  as a parameter for a class, or as a parameter for a host declaration.  To avoid creating[m
         excess broadcast traffic on your network, we recommend that you restrict the use of this option  to  as  few[m
         clients  as  possible.  For example, the Microsoft DHCP client is known not to have this problem, as are the[m
         OpenTransport and ISC DHCP clients.[m
[m
       The [4malways-reply-rfc1048[24m statement[m
[m
         [1malways-reply-rfc1048[0m [4mflag[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         Some BOOTP clients expect RFC1048-style responses, but do not follow RFC1048 when  sending  their  requests.[m
         You  can  tell that a client is having this problem if it is not getting the options you have configured for[m
         it and if you see in the server log the message  "(non-rfc1048)"  printed  with  each  BOOTREQUEST  that  is[m
         logged.[m
[m
[7m Manual page dhcpd.conf(5) line 1269/1945 67% (press h for help or q to quit)[27m[K[K/[K         If  you  want  to send rfc1048 options to such a client, you can set the [1malways-reply-rfc1048[0m option in that[m
         client's host declaration, and the DHCP server will respond with an  RFC-1048-style  vendor  options  field.[m
         This flag can be set in any scope, and will affect all clients covered by that scope.[m
[m

       The [4mddns-hostname[24m statement[m
[m

       The [4mddns-domainname[24m statement[m
[m
         [1mddns-domainname[0m [4mname[24m[1m;[0m[m
[m
         The [4mname[24m parameter should be the domain name that will be appended to the client's hostname to form a fully-[m
